==PAGE:5==
Abstract
In the automotive industry the optimisation of the after-treatment system for the exhaust
gas is of big interest because the emission standards become increasingly stricter. For
the control of the emission of soot and NOx a diesel particle ’¼ülter (DPF) and a selective
catalytic reduction (SCR) are used, respectively. Because the emission standards are
becoming more stricter, an interest in combining these two units as a combined DPF and
SCR unit has arisen.
In this thesis, an isothermal model for a DPF combined with SCR is derived and solved
for an iron-based SCR catalyst. The model is used to predict the pressure drop, NOx
conversion and thickness of the soot layer on the wall. From the simulation results it was
found that both the NOx conversion and the amount of combusted soot increase with
increasing NO2 concentration and temperature, which was expected because NO2 is the
reactant for both the fast SCR reaction and the soot combustion. And as the temperature
increases the reaction rate increases.
From the simulations, it was also found that the soot combustion are fastest in the
beginning of the channel. Thereby, the thickness of the soot layer will increase down the
channel. Overall, it was found that the NOx conversion is very high for a temperature of
600 K or higher. If the thickness of the soot layer is zero, the conversion is 93 % at 600
K for a NO/NO2 ratio of 10 and an inlet concentration of NOx at 550 ppm. If the soot
layer thickness is constant and equal to 75 ┬Ąm, the conversion decreases with 1.5 %.

Problem Statement
Project title: Modelling of catalyzed diesel particulate ’¼ülter for combined soot removal
and DeNOx
ECTS points: 30
Student: Helene Hummelmose (s113185)
Supervisors:
DTU: Prof.
Anker Degn Jensen (DTU), Asc.
Prof.
Jakob Kj├Ėbsted
Huusom, PhD student Andreas ├ģberg. Haldor Tops├Ėe: Kasper Linde
Place: CHEC (Combustion and Harmful Emission Control) research Centre, Depart-
ment of chemical engineering, DTU
Period: 01-08-2016 to 14-01-2017
Background
The emission regulations for diesel trucks are becoming more strict, and thereby more
advanced and complex after-treatment systems are needed. The modern systems that are
used today typically consist of a diesel oxidation catalyst (DOC) that oxidizes CO and
hydrocarbons to CO2 and water and NO to NO2, a diesel particulate ’¼ülter (DPF) that
retains the soot particles in the ’¼éue gas, a reactor for selective catalytic reduction (SCR)
of NOx by injecting ammonia, and ’¼ünally an ammonia slip catalyst that oxidizes surplus
ammonia to N2. Such a complex system takes up a signi’¼ücant amount of space on the
vehicle, and there is therefore an interest in combining the DPF and the SCR units to
a single unit. This will save space on the vehicle and ensure faster heating of the whole
system.
The SCR-DPF consists of a monolith with multiple channels. The channels are plugged
in alternate ends, and thereby forcing wall-through ’¼éow. The soot is deposited on the
inlet channel wall and the SCR catalyst is coated on the ’¼ülter wall. The soot deposited
on the wall gives an increase in the pressure, and to insure the pressure is kept at a
reasonable level, the ’¼ülter is regenerated by combustion of the soot using oxygen and
NO2. By combining the two units, the functionalities (SCR and ’¼ültering/soot combus-
tion) are mutually a’¼Ćected, since the NO2 is used both the fast SCR (involving both
NO and NO2) and in the soot combustion. The SCR catalyst may also have a catalytic
e’¼Ćect on the soot combustion, leading to an overall increase in the soot combustion. To
fully understand how the two units a’¼Ćect each other a mathematical model is a useful tool.
Scope
In this thesis a model of a single channel in the SCR-DPF will be developed. The model
will be used to investigate how the system react to di’¼Ćerent operating conditions. The
model will take mass and energy balances into account, the reactions for the soot com-
bustion by oxygen and NO2 and the SCR reactions. The model will account for both soot
deposition and ’¼ülter regeneration. The model may further be validated with experimental
data from in-house experiments or from the literature. The model is solved by dividing
the model into smaller parts. The parts are; (1) an isotherm ’¼éow model, (2) an isotherm
’¼éow and species model including the SCR kinetic, (3) an isotherm ’¼éow and species model
including the SCR kinetic and soot kinetic, (4) a ’¼éow, species and temperature model
v
==PAGE:10==
Content
The project will contain the following topics:
ŌĆó A literature study of the DPF, SCR and the SCR-DPF technology with focus on
current models from the literature
ŌĆó Development of a transient chemical engineering model of an SCR-DPF taking into
account all relevant physical and chemical processes. The model will be developed
by dividing the model up in smaller more simple sub-models, which are solved and
validated before adding more complexity
ŌĆó Validation of the ’¼ünal model by comparing the simulation results with experimental
data
ŌĆó Simulations using the model to understand how the SCR-’¼ülter react to di’¼Ćerent
engine operating conditions

Part I
Literature Study
1
Introduction
Air pollution has been and still is of big interest to the public. Signi’¼ücant e’¼Ćorts are
done to minimise the emission of harmful particles and gasses. One of the ’¼üelds that
are in’¼éuenced by these e’¼Ćorts is the automotive industry. In the automotive industry
the focus is on minimising the emission of CO, hydrocarbons, soot (also referred to as
particulate matter, PM), NOx (nitrogen oxides) and SO2. This thesis will focus on the
emission of soot and NOx. Due to the big interest in minimising the emission of soot and
NOx from vehicles, the global emission standards become increasingly stricter. How the
emission limits for NOx and soot have changed in the world is given in ’¼ügure 1.1 and 1.2
[1].
Figure 1.1: NOx emission standards for heavy-duty vehicles in the United States, the European
Union, Japan, and China [1].
3
==PAGE:14==
Figure 1.2: Soot emission standards for heavy-duty vehicles in the United States, the European
Union, Japan, and China [1].
To insure emission regulations are ful’¼ülled, all parts that have contact with the exhaust
gas have to be optimized. The ’¼ürst part that is of interest is the engine itself and the
fuel. The engine of course has to minimize the amount of fuel and at the same time
ensure complete combustion, so the exhaust gas becomes as clean as possible. Because
the combustion in the engine has a large in’¼éuence on the composition of the gas to the
after-treatment system, a short introduction to the diesel engine is given.
1.1
The Diesel Engine
Engines in vehicles are internal combustion engines, meaning that the combustion of the
fuel occurs in a combustion chamber that is an integral part of the working ’¼éuid ’¼éow
circuit. The engine can operate either as a two- or four-stroke, where the latter is the
case for vehicles. The strokes are: (1) intake, (2) compression (3) combustion and (4)
exhaust. The engine cycle is illustrated in ’¼ügure 1.3.
4
==PAGE:15==
Figure 1.3: Four-stroke engine cycle [2].
It is in the combustion of the fuel the exhaust gas is generated. The fuel can be either
gasoline or diesel, where this thesis will focus on diesel engines. The exact composition of
diesel varies a lot, and it is therefore not always possible to operate the engine at optimal
conditions. The diesel, however, mainly consists of hydrocarbons. When the diesel is
combusted the main product will thus be CO2 and water, according to a combustion
reaction given by:
CxHy +
 
x + y
4
!
O2 ŌłÆŌłÆŌåÆxCO2 + y
2H2O
(R1)
For a complete combustion, the exhaust gas will thus consist of CO2, H2O and air (O2
and N2). However because it is not a complete combustion the exhaust gas will also
contain CO, hydrocarbons, soot and NOx.
CO is generated when there is not enough oxygen present in a combustion reaction or if
the reaction time is too low. The generation of CO is undesirable; the combustion is thus
run at a surplus of oxygen. However, a small amount of CO is still generated, according
to the reaction below:
CxHy +
 x
2 + y
4
!
O2 ŌłÆŌłÆŌåÆxCO + y
2H2O
(R2)
Soot is one of the most characteristic diesel emissions. Soot consists of unburned organic
compounds and other solid and liquid material [3]. The composition of soot varies a
lot from test run to test run. In table 1.1 some typical compositions of soot are given,
including the composition of commercially made soot, Black Valcon 6, that are used in
some experiments [3], [4].
Table 1.1: Di’¼Ćerent composition of soot (% w/w) [4], [5].

It is important to remove soot from the gas because of the health and environmental
impact. Soot can in’¼éuence the health by inhalation of the particles. Soot can e’¼Ćect
5
==PAGE:16==
the health on short term but can also cause chronic side e’¼Ćects. Short term e’¼Ćects can
be irritation of eyes, nose and lungs, and chronic side e’¼Ćects can be as serious as lung
cancer. The size of the particles have a great in’¼éuence on how the soot a’¼Ćects the health.
The ’¼üner the particles become, the more dangerous they are because they can avoid the
human respiratory system defence mechanisms and enter deeply into the lungs [6]. The
size distribution of soot is given in ’¼ügure 1.4.
Figure 1.4: Typical size distribution of diesel exhaust particles, dp is the particle diameter [7].
From the ’¼ügure, it is seen that the small particles that make up the nuclei mode are
present in the greatest number, but only contribute little to the total mass. The larger
particles (accumulation mode) only contribute little to the total number, but dominate
the particulate mass. The amount of emitted soot is determined based on the weight of
the soot and number of particles. The amount and number of particles are determined
by taking a sample from the exhaust gas, diluting the sample in water and ’¼ültering the
sampler over a ’¼ülter. The amount of emitted soot is thus determined based on the weight
of the soot and number of particles. The result from this procedure is very sensitive and
the result can vary if e.g. a di’¼Ćerent ’¼ülter paper is used. The emission standard thus also
includes the procedure of determining the amount of emitted soot [8].
NOx is generated when nitrogen from the air is oxidized. NOx refers to all nitrogen
oxides, but the NOx in the exhaust gas is mainly NO and NO2, 95-85 % NO and 5-15 %
NO2 [4]. The emission of NOx is also a big issue, and vehicles account for approximately
20 % of the total NOx emission [9].
As for the soot, it is important to reduce NOx emission due to health and environmental
factors. NOx in’¼éuences the health by inhalation, where it can cause airway irritation and
asthma. NOx emission also plays an important role in creating acid rain, which damages
the leaves on trees and other plants [10].
The engine itself cannot ensure that the emission standards are ful’¼ülled. It is thus neces-
sary with an after-treatment system for the exhaust gas.
6
==PAGE:17==
1.2
The After-treatment System
The after-treatment system used today consists of di’¼Ćerent parts that each have di’¼Ćer-
ent tasks with respect to cleaning the exhaust gas. An illustration of a modern after-
treatment system is given in ’¼ügure 1.5. In the ’¼ügure it is seen that the after-treatment
system consists of a diesel oxidation catalyst (DOC), a diesel particle ’¼ülter (DPF), a
selective catalytic reduction (SCR) and an ammonia slip catalyst (ASC).
Figure 1.5: An illustration of the after-treatment system.
In the DOC the CO and hydrocarbon are oxidized to CO2 and water. The DOC also
oxidizes some of the NO to NO2 which is used in the regeneration of the DPF and in the
SCR [11], [12]. An undesirable oxidation is oxidation of SO2 to SO3, because SO3 leads
to sulphate particle that increase the particulate emission. The DOC is thus designed
so the oxidation of CO and hydrocarbon are high and at the same time the oxidation of
SO2 is low [13].
The DPF captures the soot and has been used for close to three decades based on the
high e’¼āciency that almost reaches 100 % [14]. The ’¼ülter is regenerated by combustion
of the soot. A detailed description of the DPF is given in section 2.
The SCR is used to reduce the NOx to N2, using urea or NH3 as reduction agent. The
e’¼āciency of this part is highly dependent on the NO to NO2 ratio and the NOx to NH3
ratio. The SCR is described in section 3.
The ammonia slip is a term describing the unreacted ammonia from the SCR. The unre-
acted ammonia occurs when ammonia is over-injected, the temperature is too low or a fast
raise in the temperature occurs. Unreacted ammonia can also escape if the catalyst in the
SCR is degraded. To minimize the emission of ammonia an ammonia slip catalyst is used.
The catalyst oxidizes the ammonia to nitrogen, thereby avoiding secondary emissions [15].
This thesis will only focus on the DPF and the SCR units. A detailed description of
these two units is therefore given below. Due to the more strict emission relegations,
there is a need for more a complex after-treatment system. An interest has thus arisen
in combining the DPF and SCR to one unit, SCR-DPF. A combination of the two units
will result in faster heating of the system while space is saved. The heating issue mostly
concerns the SCR, and is discussed in section 4. By making the after-treatment system
more compact it is possible to have a larger ’¼ülter and catalyst area, and thereby remove
more soot and NOx.
The focus will be on modelling the ’¼ülter. The modelling of the ’¼ülter will be divided
in smaller sub-models. This is done to simplify the process. The model will be divided in
the following sub-models: (1) an isothermal ’¼éow model, (2) an isothermal ’¼éow and spe-
cies model including the SCR kinetic, (3) an isotherm ’¼éow and species model including
7
==PAGE:18==
the SCR kinetic and soot kinetic. However, to be able to develop a model it is important
to understand the physical and chemicals processes of the system.
8
==PAGE:19==
2
DPF Technology
As mentioned in the introduction, soot is removed from the exhaust gas. The newest
emission regulations for heavy-duty vehicles in Europe came with EURO VI, which de-
creased the permitted amount of soot to 16 mg/kWh [16]. Soot is removed from the
exhaust gas using a ’¼ülter. The DPF that is used today is a wall-’¼éow monolith, due to its
high e’¼āciency of approximately 97 % [17], [14], [18]. The DPF is designed as a cylinder
with rectangular channels (see ’¼ügure 2.1a). The DPF works by having inlet and exit
channels, both plugged in alternate ends, thereby forcing the gas to go through the walls
due to pressure and di’¼Ćusion. The soot is thus retained in and on the ’¼ülter wall. The
velocity pro’¼üle and the channels for such a ’¼ülter are seen in ’¼ügure 2.1b
(a) The DPF monolith [19]
(b) Velocity for one inlet and one outlet change for a
diesel particle ’¼ülter [14].
Figure 2.1: Design of a DPF.
The soot is deposited on the ’¼ülter by three di’¼Ćerent mechanisms: (a) Brownian di’¼Ćusion,
(b) interception and (c) inertial [20]. The mechanisms are illustrated in ’¼ügure 2.2. Each
mechanism is the main factor for collecting di’¼Ćerent kinds of particles.
The Brownian di’¼Ćusion is the main mechanism for capturing small particles. The particles
are caught by the particles di’¼Ćusion away from the streamlines, and then caught by the
collector unit. The e’¼āciency for the Brownian di’¼Ćusion is determined from the Peclet
number, which relates to the velocity with the di’¼Ćusion coe’¼ācient [20].
The interception is when the particle does not deviate from the streamlines. The particles
are caught if the particle and the collector unit intercept. The main factor for e’¼āciency
is thus the diameter of the particles and collector unit [20].
The last mechanism is inertial. Inertial is when the large particles leave the stream-
lines. The particles are ’¼ünally collected with the collector unit by impaction [20].
9
==PAGE:20==
Figure 2.2: The three di’¼Ćerent deposited mechanisms [20].
Each mechanism and their e’¼āciency factor will not be included in the model developed
later on. The amount of soot deposited on the ’¼ülter is an important factor, as it in’¼éuences
the pressure and ’¼éow resistance. How the pressure will change with the amount of soot
deposited on the ’¼ülter is given in ’¼ügure 2.3.
Figure 2.3: The pressure di’¼Ćerence as function of amount of soot accumulated on the ’¼ülter
[21].
As it is seen in the ’¼ügure the pressure increases fastest in the beginning (region 1 and 2),
where afterwards a linear increase of the pressure with the amount of soot is seen (region
3). The linear increase in pressure is due to the formation of the cake layer that is also
seen in ’¼ügure 2.1b. The non-linear increase is when the soot is deposited in the wall,
called deep bed ’¼ültration [21], [14], [22], [20].
At some point, the pressure in the ’¼ülter becomes too high. At worst case, the exhaust
gas cannot leave the system, which can damage the engine. To insure that this is avoided
the ’¼ülter is regenerated.
10
==PAGE:21==
2.1
Regeneration
The regeneration of the ’¼ülter occurs by combustion of the soot. The combustion can
be done either uncatalyzed or catalysed, where oxygen and NO2 are used as oxidation
agents. The reaction scheme for the combustion is given by reaction R3-R8 [3].
C + O2 ŌłÆŌłÆŌåÆCO2
(R3)
C + 1
2O2 ŌłÆŌłÆŌåÆCO
(R4)
C + 2 NO2 ŌłÆŌłÆŌåÆCO2 + 2 NO
(R5)
C + NO2 ŌłÆŌłÆŌåÆCO + NO
(R6)
C + NO2 + 1
2O2 ŌłÆŌłÆŌåÆCO2 + NO
(R7)
C + 1
2O2 ( + NO2) ŌłÆŌłÆŌåÆCO ( + NO2)
(R8)
Reaction R3 and R4 are the direct oxidation of soot by oxygen and reaction R5 and
R6 are the direct oxidation by NO2. The last two reactions (reaction R7 and R8) are
cooperative reactions where both oxygen and NO2 are used.
NO2 is the most reactive of the two oxidation agents, and the oxidation reactions with
NO2 occur typically at a temperature between 250-400 CŌŚ”. However because there is
more oxygen present in the exhaust gas, the soot oxidation by oxygen plays an important
rule, these oxidation reactions occur when the temperature is above 600 CŌŚ”[4]. However,
the temperature for the oxidation with oxygen is decreased when using the catalyst.
The decrease in reaction temperature when the catalyst is used, is important because
the temperature of the ’¼ülter is typical between 200-500 ŌŚ”C [23]. For the uncatalyzed case
the temperature thus need to be increased to occur, this is done with injection of fuel
upstream the DOC [23]. The need of a temperature increase can thus be avoided by a
use of a catalyst. In ’¼ügure 2.4 it is seen how the catalyst and non-catalyst behave at
di’¼Ćerent temperatures for two di’¼Ćerent catalysts.
In the ’¼ügure the di’¼Ćerence between tight and loose contact is also seen. The tight and
loose contact are de’¼üned by how the soot and catalyst are mixed. It was Neeft et al.
[24] who developed the two mixing method to be used to distinguish between how the
catalyst and soot are in contact [5]. In the ’¼ürst method the soot and the catalyst were
mixed with a spatula, loose contact. In the second method the mixture was mechanically
milled for an hour, referred to as tight contact [5]. Neeft et al. [24] tested many di’¼Ćerent
catalysts for the soot combustion, and found that there in most cases were a big di’¼Ćerence
in burning rate for the two methods. In all cases, the tight contact had a higher burning
rate than the loose contact. In some cases the loose contact and the combustion of soot
without catalyst had almost the same burning rate [24]. In ’¼ügure 2.4 the burning rates
for tight and loose contact for a Fe2O3 catalyzed and a MoO3 catalyzed soot oxidation
are seen.
11
==PAGE:22==
(a) Tight and loose contact mode Fe2O3
catalyzed soot oxidation.
(b) Tight and loose contact mode MoO3
catalyzed soot oxidation.
Figure 2.4: Burning rates of soot for tight and loose contact [24].
The burning rates of soot as function of the temperature is found by a thermograti-
metrical analysis with use of the model based soot Printex-U. The weight ratio between
the soot and the catalyst was 2:1 [24]. From the ’¼ügure, it is seen that the iron-based
catalyst has a low catalytic e’¼Ćect when the soot and the catalyst is in loose contact, but
has a high e’¼Ćect for a tight contact. The molybdenum based catalyst is also in’¼éuenced
by the tight versus loose contact, but still has a catalytic e’¼Ćect on the oxidation of soot
also in loose contact. The soot in the DPF is in tight contact with the catalyst, and it is
thus the kinetic parameter for tight contact that should be used in the model.
This tight versus loose contact, and the fact that many di’¼Ćerent catalysts can be used
imply that many di’¼Ćerent rate constants can be found in the literature. Furthermore,
water also has a catalytic e’¼Ćect on the rate [4], [5]. Nabila et al. investigated the rate
constant and waterŌĆÖs in’¼éuence on the rate constant for the non-catalytic case [3]. They
found the reaction rate with use of the commercially made soot Black Valcon 6. The
experiments were performed in a ’¼üxed bed reactor at 1 atm where the temperature was
kept constant. They wrote the rate of soot combustion as [3]:

For the catalytic soot combustion, the soot and the catalyst have to be in contact. The
cake layer is on top of the wall, and it is thus only a thin layer of the cake layer, which
is in contact with the catalyst. To account for the contact between the soot and the
catalyst, the two-layer model can be used.
In the two-layer model, the cake layer is divided in to two parts. The model is illus-
trated in ’¼ügure 2.5.
Figure 2.5: Schematic illustration of the two-layer model [25].
Layer I is the catalyst a’¼Ćected layer. However, it is only a fraction of the soot that is
a’¼Ćected by the catalyst, the fraction that is a’¼Ćected is given by ╬▓. ╬▓ depends on the
’¼ülter and coating structure [25].
Layer II it is only the non-catalyst combustion that takes place. Some of the particles
from this layer can move to layer I, and thus be a’¼Ćected by the catalyst. The fraction
that moves to layer I is given by the interaction parameter, ╬Š. ╬Š also depends on the ’¼ülter
and coating structure [25].
13
==PAGE:24==
3
SCR Technology
NOx from exhaust gas is typically reduced over a selective catalytic reduction (SCR)
catalyst. SCR is often used due to its broad operating window; it can operated at low
temperature and is still stable at high temperatures. Furthermore, SCR has a reasonable
cost and available infrastructure [26].
One of the challenges with the SCR technology is the temperature. This has increased
even more, with the focus on minimizing the CO2 emission and thereby boosting fuel
economy, which lowers the temperature for the exhaust gas. This requires that the SCR
has a high e’¼āciency at low temperatures. Especially cold-starts and urban driving or
other low load conditions challenge the SCR due to the low temperatures. In these cases,
the temperature of the exhaust gas is below 200 ŌŚ”C, which is too low for the SCR system
[26].
3.1
Selection of Reduction Agent
For the catalytic reduction of NOx a reduction agent is needed, which is typically urea
which are decomposes to ammonia. Due to safety reason ammonia cannot be used in its
pure form [27], instead an ammonia carrier is used. The selective criteria for the ammonia
carrier is that decomposition or desorption species should be in a low concentration and
should be species that already exist in the exhaust gas. The volume and mass density
of NH3 should be high, and the decomposition or desorption temperature should not
be too high [28]. With these criteria, generally three di’¼Ćerent sources of NH3 have the
best potential. These are urea, ammonium salt and metal ammines [26], [28]. Both the
ammonium salts and metal ammines shows better potential [9], [28], however urea is still
the preferred reduction agent [26]. Therefore urea will shortly be discussed.
Urea is an organic compound that is used as a fertilizer and feed supplement.
The
chemical formula for urea is (NH2)2CO [29]. Urea has a good potential due to safety and
low toxicity [26]. Urea however also has many disadvantages. Urea freezes at ŌłÆ11 ŌŚ”C,
urea can thus not be used all over the world [9]. Even in countries like Denmark this
gives some problems during the winter season [30], [31]. Urea also has the disadvantage
that it slowly decomposes in the storage tank when the temperature is above 50-60 ŌŚ”C
[9]. Furthermore, urea does not convert completely if the temperature is under 200 ŌŚ”C
[26]. Another disadvantage with urea is that it has to be relativity pure, to minimize the
build-up of impurities in the SCR catalyst. The dissolution that is used is thus made
from pure urea and distilled water [9].
Even though there are many disadvantages linked to the use of urea as ammonia source,
urea is still widely used. Urea is added as an aqueous solution in a separate tank where it
is added to the after-treatment system. Before the urea can be used in the after-treatment
system it decomposes into ammonia. The decomposition happens in three steps [26].
The ’¼ürst step is evaporation of water [26]. The evaporation of the water decreases the
temperature of the exhaust gas by 10-15 ŌŚ”C. This in’¼éuences the activity, especially the
SCR for cold starts [9]. The reaction equation is given by [26];
(NH2)2CO(aq) ŌłÆŌłÆŌåÆ(NH2)2CO(molten) + H2O(g)
(R9)
14
==PAGE:25==
The second step is thermal decomposition of urea, where urea decomposes to NH3 and
HNCO. The reaction for this decomposition is given by [26];
(NH2)2CO(molten) ŌłÆŌłÆŌåÆNH3(g) + HNCO(g)
(R10)
The third and last step is hydrolysis of HNCO to NH3 and CO2. This is a catalytic
reaction. The catalytic reaction can occur before the SCR reactor or it can be part of
the SCR reaction [26], [9]. The reaction equation is [26];
HNCO(g) + H2O(g) ŌłÆŌłÆŌåÆNH3(g) + CO2(g)
(R11)
The overall reaction for the decomposition of urea is thus [26];
(NH2)2CO + H2O ŌłÆŌłÆŌåÆ2NH3 + CO2
(R12)
For the model developed in this thesis it will be assumed that the decomposition of the
reduction agent is fast, compared to the SCR reduction of NOx. The decomposition of
the reduction agent to NH3 will thus not be included in the model. The reaction of the
NOx reduction is given in the section below.
3.2
Reaction Scheme for the NOx Reduction
The reaction scheme for the SCR reduction of NOx that will be used in this study is
given by the following seven reactions [12]:
NH3(g) ŌłÆŌłÆŌćĆ
ŌåĮŌłÆŌłÆNH3(ads)
(R13)
4NH3(ads) + 4NO + O2 ŌłÆŌłÆŌåÆ4N2 + 6H2O
(R14)
2 NH3(ads) + NO + NO2 ŌłÆŌłÆŌåÆ2N2 + 3 H2O
(R15)
4 NH3(ads) + 2 NO2 ŌłÆŌłÆŌåÆ31
2N2 + 6H2O
(R16)
4 NH3(ads) + 3O2 ŌłÆŌłÆŌåÆ2N2 + 6H2O
(R17)
4 NH3(ads) + 5O2 ŌłÆŌłÆŌåÆ4 NO + 6H2O
(R18)
NO2 ŌłÆŌłÆŌćĆ
ŌåĮŌłÆŌłÆNO + 1
2O2
(R19)
Reaction R13 is the adsorption and desorption of NH3 on the catalyst surface. Because
it is a catalytic reaction, only the absorbed NH3 (NH3(abs)) reacts with NOx. Reaction
R14 to R16 are the reduction of the NOx. Reaction R14 is the reduction of NO, the
standard SCR reaction. Reaction R15 is the fast SCR reaction and reaction R16 is the
reduction of NO2, the slow SCR reaction. Reaction R17 and R18 are the oxidation reac-
tion of NH3, and is thus undesirable reactions. Reaction R19 is the equilibrium reaction
between NO and NO2. Depending on the type of catalyst, there can also be reactions
that generated N2O [32].
15
==PAGE:26==
How many of these reactions that are included when the SCR unit is modelled, varies
throughout the literature. The standard SCR (reaction R14) is always included, because
it is the main reaction. It is the main reaction because the concentration of NO is much
higher than the concentration of NO2 (5-15 % of the NOx is NO2 [4]). The standard SCR
reaction will thus be the dominating SCR reaction.
16
==PAGE:27==
4
DPF Combined with SCR
Due to the stricter emission regulations, an interest in combining the DPF and the SCR
to one unit has grown. The two units are combined by coating the SCR catalyst on the
DPF wall. The advantage of combining these is that the packaging volume and costs de-
crease. The reduction in packaging volume is of interest because its gives a more ’¼éexible
packaging, and because an increase of the SCR volume can be needed due to the stricter
emission limits on NOx reduction. It has been shown that a 40-55 % reduction in the
after-treatment volume can be achieved by combining the two units [17]. A combination
of the two units also improves the problem with SCR at cold starts, as mentioned in
section 3. If the two systems are combined the SCR will come closer to the engine and
thereby the temperature will increase [12] [17].
A combination of the two units have, however, also given some problems, and there
are still some challenges with this system. When the two units are combined the design
of the system has to be optimal for both the ’¼ültration, regeneration and the SCR reac-
tions, instead of only one of the units. Furthermore the modelling and control have also
given some problems [17] [12], [17], the scope of this thesis is the modelling. The two
parts also in’¼éuence each other, and how the two parts a’¼Ćect each other is explained in
the following two sections.
4.1
SCR In’¼éuence on the Soot Oxidation Rate
The SCR reactions a’¼Ćect the oxidations of soot in the ’¼ülter and thereby the pressure [17].
That the SCR reactions in’¼éuence the oxidation rate is expected as the concentration of
the oxidation agent is reduced by the SCR reactions. The oxidation agent for the regen-
eration of the ’¼ülter is NO2 and oxygen (see reaction R3-R8). NO2 is reduced to N2 in
the SCR reactions (see reaction R15, R16 and R19).
How the SCR reactions in’¼éuence the oxidation of soot can be investigated by simulations
and experiments where the same system and condition is investigated for an ammonia
to NOx ratio (ANR) of 0 and larger than 0. For an ANR of zero the SCR reactions will
not occur, and it can thus be used as a baseline for the oxidation without SCR. In this
study, ANRŌĆÖs and temperatureŌĆÖs in’¼éuence on the oxidation of soot is investigated.
In ’¼ügure 4.1 it is shown how the ANR in’¼éuences the soot oxidation rate. In the experi-
ment, the ’¼ülter was coated with a Cu-zeolite catalyst and the exhaust gas was generated
by an engine. From the ’¼ügure it is seen that a high ANR gives a low soot oxidation
rate (see by the high pressure drop). This is also expected as the NO2 concentration will
decrease with increasing ANR, because a higher ANR gives a faster NOx reduction.
17
==PAGE:28==
Figure 4.1: Pressure in the ’¼ülter as function of time for di’¼Ćerent ANR [15].
The temperatureŌĆÖs in’¼éuence on the soot oxidation rate is illustrated by ’¼ügure 4.2. The
results on the ’¼ügure were obtained by simulation for a Cu-SCRF catalyst with an initial
soot loading of 5 g/L. In the ’¼ügure it is seen that at high temperatures (above 450 ŌŚ”C)
the SCR reactions do not in’¼éuence the oxidation rate, but at low temperatures they do.
That the soot oxidation rate is more or less the same for high temperatures is because
the oxidation with oxygen (reaction R3 and R4) is more dominant [33], and the oxygen
concentration changes less than the NO2 concentration by the SCR reactions.
Figure 4.2: Soot removal as function of temperature after 60 min for temperatures lower than
400 ŌŚ”C and after 5 min for temperature above 400 ŌŚ”C in the presence of ammonia (ANR = 1)
and the absence of ammonia (ANR = 0) [33].
4.2
Soot LoadingŌĆÖs In’¼éuence on SCR Conversion
In the above section, it is mentioned how the SCR reactions in’¼éuence the soot oxidation
rate, but the soot loading also in’¼éuences the NOx conversion. In ’¼ügure 4.3 the conversion
for di’¼Ćerent NO2/NOx ratios is investigated for 0 and 5 g/L soot on the ’¼ülter for a Cu-
zeolite catalyst.
18
==PAGE:29==
Figure 4.3: Nox conversion for di’¼Ćerent soot loading and NO2/NOx ratio [34].
In the ’¼ügure it is seen that the soot loading both has a positive and a negative e’¼Ćect on
the NOx conversion for di’¼Ćerent ratios of NO2/NOx [35]. A NO2/NOx ratio at zero is
not in’¼éuenced by the soot loading, this is because it is the NO2 that is used in both the
SCR reactions and the soot reaction. If the ratio is 0.5, the soot loading however has
a negative in’¼éuence on the NOx conversion. This is because the ideal NO2/NOx ratio
for the SCR reaction is 0.5 [17], some of the NO2 is used in the soot reaction leading to
a higher consumption of NO2 and thereby the ratio needs to be higher before the ideal
value is reached. For a ratio of 1 an increase in NOx conversion is seen, due to the ratio
coming closer to the ideal value for the SCR when the NO2 is oxidized to NO in the soot
combustion [17].
That the two systems in’¼éuence each other is of course important, and is one of the
disadvantages of combining the two systems. However, the advantages are many more
and this is still a promising technology to improve the after-treatment system for exhaust
gas. The oxidation rate is only in’¼éuenced due to a decrease in concentrations. It is thus
easy to account for in the mathematical model, because by introducing both sets of re-
action equations (the soot oxidation reactions and the NOx reduction reaction equation)
the reduction of the concentration will be calculated.
19
==PAGE:30==
5
Mathematical Model of the DPF-SCR
Mathematical modelling is an important tool for system understanding. A mathematical
model is also useful when a system needs to be designed, optimized and controlled. The
bene’¼üt of using a mathematical model is that it is time and cost e’¼Ćective. When the
mathematical model is made it is easy to simulate many di’¼Ćerent scenarios, and thereby
investigate how the system react to di’¼Ćerent operation conditions, however, a model can-
not stand alone. A model should thus always be validated with experimental data.
Before the model for the DPF-SCR system is discussed, the coordinate system that
will be used is presented. The coordinate system and some of the dimensions are given
in ’¼ügure 5.1.
Figure 5.1: The coordinate system used in the model and some characteristic lengths [14].
There are already many models in the literature for the DPF-SCR system, especially for
the DPF unit. The models for the DPF unit can however easily be used for the DPF-
SCR, as SCR only in’¼éuences the reaction term. The models that are available all take
di’¼Ćerent assumptions; however, some of the assumptions are the same, and will also be
used in this thesis. The general assumptions are:
1. The gas is ideal.
2. Laminar ’¼éow in the channels.
3. The ’¼éow in the solid layer will only happen in the y direction
4. A single inlet and outlet channel represents the behaviour of all inlet and outlet
channels.
5. Combustion of soot only occurs in the ’¼ülter cake.
6. The density of the ’¼ülter cake is constant and soot combustion thus occurs by shrink-
age of the cake thickness.
The assumptions about the laminar ’¼éow and that the velocity in the solid layer is only
in the y direction (assumptions 2 and 3) are introduced to reduce the dimension, and
thereby simplify the model. In the channel, it will be assumed that there is no-slip at
both the boundaries, even though this is not the case due to the porous wall. Further-
more, an average velocity will be used for the channel.
20
==PAGE:31==
For assumption 3 to be valid, the gas that enters the unit has to be spatially uniform and
the walls perfectly isolated. This can be approached by proper design of the unit and the
upstream pipe [19].
The combustion of the soot will occur both in the cake layer and in the wall. How-
ever, the combustion of the soot in the wall layer is neglected. The only reaction in the
wall is thus the SCR reactions.
The models that are discussed in the following sections all use these assumptions. The
models also have that in common that they are based on BissettŌĆÖs channel model. This
model will thus also be presented in this thesis. The model is also presented because it
gives a good introduction to how the system is modelled and how the assumptions are
used.
5.1
BissettŌĆÖs Channel Model
Bissett and Shadman (1985) initiated the modern path of DPF modelling, and in 1985
they published their model for regeneration of the ’¼ülter [14]. In the model they assumed
that there was only change through the solid phase, and neglected the change through
the channels [36]. This assumption is not used in todayŌĆÖs model. The conclusion from
their work is however used to set up the standard channel model that is used today [14].
Most of the models today are based on BissettŌĆÖs channel model, which is a follow up
on the model made by Bissett and Shadman 1. BissettŌĆÖs model is developed from the as-
sumptions given above. In addition to those assumptions, Bissett also used the following
assumptions [19]:
1. The thickness of the soot layer is very thin compared to the channel. Change in
diameter due to the soot layer can thus be neglected.
2. The emitted particles do not interfere with the ’¼éow due to their low mass fraction
within the bulk gas.
3. Quasi steady state for the temperature and velocity in the channel.
4. Neglect the thin heat transfer layer in the wall requiring all heat transfer to take
place in the channels. The wall is thereby isothermal.
5. Assume that the velocity and density of the gas are the same in the solid layer.
BissettŌĆÖs channel model will be introduced by going through each set of balance for the
system.
5.1.1
Continuity Equations
The change in mass ’¼éow rate in the z direction in the inlet channel is due to the gas
transport through the wall. The continuity equation for the inlet channel can thus be
written as:

at the wall. It will be assumed that the friction force should be close to that observed

5.1.3
Energy Balance
The energy balance is developed by neglecting the following factors: (1) accumulation (2)
viscous e’¼Ćects (3) pressure di’¼Ćerences in the y direction, (4) conduction in the y direction
and (5) kinetic energy, due to it is assumed that u2 is much smaller than the enthalpy.

5.1.4

In the mass balance for the ’¼ülter, the change in thickness of the cake layer is accounted
for. The change in thickness is given by the amount of added soot minus the amount
of soot removed by reaction. Bissett only considers the amount of soot removed. The
change in thickness can thus be written as [19]:

The total rate of soot combustion is given by the amount of oxygen in minus the amount
of oxygen out. Bissett assume that the fraction of oxygen in the channel is equal to the
fraction of oxygen in the inlet (YO2(0, t)). The amount out of the system as function of
the inlet fraction is found by assuming that the reaction is ’¼ürst order. The total rate of

following the di’¼Ćerent types of equations in the same order BissettŌĆÖs channel model was

5.3
Summary of the Di’¼Ćerent Models and Assumptions
A summary of the models that are introduced in the section above is given in table 5.1.
In the table, it is seen that the main di’¼Ćerences between the models are how the following
parameters are calculated:
1. Velocity in the solid layer
2. Temperature for the solid phase and the gas in the solid layer
3. The concentration of the species in the channel and solid layer
4. Reaction term
5. Change in diameter of the inlet channel
The di’¼Ćerences in the models are thus mainly for the calculation of the parameter for
the solid layer. The model is thus easily simpli’¼üed by use of an average parameter for
the solid layer. However, because the reaction occurs in the solid layer, the parameter
for this area of the system is thus also very important. How the simpli’¼ücations in’¼éuence
the simulation result is therefore investigated.
The in’¼éuence of using an average velocity for the solid layer is investigated by use of
the results from Depcik and Assanis. They solved the model equation where the mass
’¼éow rate is a function of y [38]. The result is illustrated in ’¼ügure 5.2.
Figure 5.2: The velocity and pressure through the solid layer for three di’¼Ćerent soot loadings;
0.00 g, 83.31 g and 174.37 g [38].
29
==PAGE:40==
From the ’¼ügure, it is seen that for a clean ’¼ülter the velocity and pressure are almost
constant in the solid layer, where for a ’¼ülter loaded with 174.37 g soot a larger change is
seen. This is also what is expected as the resistance in the ’¼ülter increases with increased
loading. The operating conditions of the ’¼ülter thus have a large in’¼éuence on how the
assumptions a’¼Ćect the result. From the above result Depcik and Assanis concluded that
for normal conditions it is reasonable to assume that the pressure and velocity can be
calculated as average values [38], [14].
The result from Depcik and Assanis can also be used to investigate the assumption
of the temperature of the gas in the solid layer and the solid itself. The result is given
in ’¼ügure 5.3. The results are for warm-up of the ’¼ülter where the stream only contains
nitrogen, therefore no reaction occurs. In the ’¼ügure three di’¼Ćerent simulation cases are
illustrated: (1) Packed Bed Correlation, where the heat transfer coe’¼ācient is calculated
from dimensionless number, (2) Ts = Tw = Tf, where it is assumed that the temperature
of the ’¼ülter and gas in the ’¼ülter is equal and (3) Altered Correlation, where the heat
transfer coe’¼ācient is ’¼ütted to the data.
Figure 5.3: The temperature of the solid layer during warm-up at di’¼Ćerent positions [38].
From the ’¼ügure, it is seen that the temperature only slightly deviates from the experi-
mental data for all three cases. In the case of warm-up it is thus a good assumption that
the gas in the solid and the solid layer have the same temperature. However, from this
result it cannot fully be concluded that this is the case for all operation conditions.
Haralampous et. al. did not include the energy balance, as their focus was on the species
balance. They solved their model with and without the di’¼Ćusion term to investigate how
the di’¼Ćusion in’¼éuences the result. They did not compare the results with experimental
data, but the results can still be used to investigate the in’¼éuence of the di’¼Ćusion. In
’¼ügure 5.4 the concentration of NO2 in the inlet and outlet channel is given. In the ’¼ügure
the concentration of NO2 in two di’¼Ćerent positions in the ’¼ülter is also given.
30
==PAGE:41==
(a) Concentration of NO2 in the inlet channel.
(b) Concentration of NO2 in the outlet chan-
nel.
(c) Concentration of NO2 in the ’¼ülter near ’¼ül-
ter entrance.
(d) Concentration of NO2 in the ’¼ülter near ’¼ül-
ter exit.
Figure 5.4: The investigation of the di’¼Ćusion in’¼éuence on the concentration of NO2 [22].
From the ’¼ügure it is seen that the di’¼Ćusion has a large in’¼éuence on the concentration
of NO2 in the inlet channel, where for the outlet channel it only lowers the concentra-
tion slightly but both curves are linear. For the inlet channel the concentration of NO2
decreases exponentially and goes to zero when the end of the channel is reached for the
case where the di’¼Ćusion is included. When the di’¼Ćusion is not included, the concentra-
tion is constant. It can thus be concluded that it is important to include the di’¼Ćusion
to calculate the concentration of the species in the channel. From the result it can also
be concluded, that the assumption that concentration of the species in the channel is
constant is not valid. For the concentration in the ’¼ülter, the di’¼Ćusion has less in’¼éuence,
but is still an important factor, especially for the soot layer.
Depcik and Assanis also investigated the assumption about calculating the area as being
constant. They did it by solving the continuity and momentum balance where the dia-
meter was taken into account and when it was not. The results are illustrated in ’¼ügure
5.5, where the percent error is the error from experimental results.
31
==PAGE:42==
Figure 5.5: Percent error of pressure drop from experimental data for assuming that dm << d
(simple) and by including the change in area (conserved) [38].
From the ’¼ügure it is seen that by increasing the thickness of the soot layer compared to
the channel diameter, the di’¼Ćerence between the two models becomes larger. This is also
expected due to the assumption that dm << d becomes less valid. It is also seen that
for a soot layer of 10 % of the channel diameter the error is 9 % for the simple model
and 6 % for the model where the change in area is included. If the soot layer is 10 % of
the channel diameter, the soot loading is 8 g/L, this correspond to a medium to heavy
loading that is avoided in actual operation. Depcik and Assanis therefore concluded that
the change in area can be neglected, as long as the model is used for normal operation
condition [38].

6
Sub-conclusion
Removal of harmful particles and gasses from the exhaust gas from vehicles is important,
due to health and environmental issues. Soot and NOx are two of the species that are re-
moved. Soot is removed by a DPF and NOx is reduced to N2 with a SCR catalyst. These
two are normally two separate units, but because the emission regulations are becoming
more strict an interest in combining the two units is growing. A combination of the two
units is of interest as it is cost and space saving, and a combination will also ensure faster
heating of the after-treatment system. The disadvantages by combining the systems are
that they in’¼éuence each other, evidenced by the fact that they use the same reactant;
NO2 is used in the combustion of soot and in the fast SCR reaction. The disadvantage
is also that by combining the two, the system has to be optimized according to two both
technologies.
The modelling of the system also becomes more complex, as the system has more func-
tionality that has to be included in the model. It is thus an advantage to divide the
model in smaller sub-models, that each can be validated with experimental data. In the
end, the complete model will account for temperature, ’¼éow and species concentrations.
From the investigation of the models in the literature, it is found that the temperature
of the solid and the gas in the solid can be assumed equal. For the ’¼éow it is found that
the change in area due to the soot is deposited on the inlet channel can be neglected.
With regard to species balance it is found that the concentration in the channel cannot
be assumed constant and the di’¼Ćusion is important.
34
==PAGE:45==
Part II
Models and Simulations Results
In this part the models used in this thesis are derived and solved. The ’¼ürst model that are
derived and solved is a ’¼éow model, where the SCR and soot kinetic are added afterwards.
All the models will be solved by looking at a single inlet and outlet channel. This is
done by assuming that the ’¼éow from one inlet channel goes to one outlet channel. In
reality the mass ’¼éow from one inlet channel is distributed to four outlet channels, but
each outlet channel has an input from four inlet channels. It can thus be assumed that
the ’¼éow from one inlet channel goes to one outlet channel.
7
Flow Model
A ’¼éow model is a model where only the total mass ’¼éow rate and pressure are considered.
This is the ’¼ürst model because the result is needed in the rest of the models. Furthermore,
the result from this model can also be useful, as it describes the velocity and pressure in
the system.
7.1
Model Derivation
Continuity and momentum balances are used to calculate the velocity and pressure. Both
balances are assumed in steady state. The control volumes, which are used in deriving of
the model, are given in ’¼ügure 7.1. In the ’¼ügure the boundary conditions are also showed.
(a) The whole system, where the grey part is the wall.
(b) A zoom in on the wall.
Figure 7.1: The system with the control volumes and boundary conditions. The blue parts
are the system and boundary conditions, and the black parts are the coordinate system. P is
the pressure, u the velocity, d the diameter and Gin the volumetric ’¼éow rate into the system.
35
==PAGE:46==
From the ’¼ügure, it is seen that the two channels have a ’¼éow in the z direction. The
inlet channel also has a ’¼éow from the channel to the wall, and the outlet channel has a
’¼éow from the wall to the channel. On ’¼ügure 7.1b it is seen that only the ’¼éow in the y
direction is considered. In reality there will also be a ’¼éow in the z direction, but this will
be neglected, because it is assumed to be small compared to the ’¼éow in the y direction.
7.1.1
Continuity Balance
Inlet Channel

at z = z, an output at z + Ōłåz and some mass will go through the wall as seen in ’¼ügure
7.1a. The continuity balance can thus be written as:

7.2
Solution Strategy
It is initially decided to use the assumption of the constant density. The system is thus
given by equation (7.3), (7.7), (7.11), (7.14), (7.16) and (7.18). It is decided to use the
constant density because it will simplify the calculations and implementations for a ’¼ürst
validation of the equations and solution procedure. The assumption of a constant density
is discussed in section 7.3.4.
From the equations, it is seen that they are ordinary di’¼Ćerential equations (ODEs) and
can thus be solved in Matlab with a build in ode solver. However, the equations are not
solved with an ode solver, because the energy balance will be partial di’¼Ćerential equations
(PDEs), because the temperature changes with position and time. The model equations
are therefore solved by discretizing the system. The method used is Method of Lines
where only the space is discretized while an ODE solver will be used to solve for the time
dimension. The discretisation of the space is shown in ’¼ügure 7.2.
(a) The whole system, where the grey part is the wall.
(b) A zoom in on the wall.
Figure 7.2: The system where the space dimensions are discretized. The blue parts are the
system and the black parts are the coordinate system and grid points.
As seen from the ’¼ügure, the channels are divided in N + 1 grid points and the wall is
divided in N ┬Ę (Nw + 1) grid points. Each grid point has an equation that calculates the
velocity and one that calculates the pressure. 4+2N of the pressures and velocities are
de’¼üned from the boundary conditions given in equation (7.19)-(7.24):
39
==PAGE:50==
uI(i = 0) = Gin
d2
I
(7.25)
uI(i = N) = 0
(7.26)
uII(i = 0) = 0
(7.27)
PII(i = N) = Patm
(7.28)
Pw(r = 0) = PI
(7.29)
Pw(r = Nw) = PII
(7.30)
The rest of the pressures and velocities are calculated from the continuity and momentum
balance. For the channel there will thus be 2N equations and for the wall (N + 1) ┬Ę 2Nw
equations.
7.2.1

For all the discretization, it is decided to use backward discretization. The di’¼Ćerential
term is thus calculated by the di’¼Ćerence in the variables at i = i and i = i ŌłÆ1.

With these variables the result is as given in ’¼ügure 7.3.
(a) The pressure in channel.
(b) Velocity in the channel and the wall.
Figure 7.3: The pressure and velocity in the system. Red dash line is for the inlet channel,
blue dotted line for the outlet channel, green line is the sum of the velocities in the inlet and
outlet channel, uI + uII, and black line is for the wall. Gin = 8.56 ┬Ę 10ŌłÆ6 m3/s.
In ’¼ügure 7.3a the pressures are given. Both the pressure in inlet and outlet channel de-
crease exponentially, where the gradient becomes zero at z = L for the inlet and at z = 0
for the outlet. The gradient reaches zero because the velocity is zero at these points, and
thereby the force due to friction is zero. The velocity is highest in the beginning of the
inlet channel and therefore the pressure gradient for the inlet decreases. The opposite is
the case for the outlet channel, where the gradient increases with z. From the ’¼ügure, it
43
==PAGE:54==
is also seen that the outlet pressure is 1 atm, that is one of the boundary conditions.
In ’¼ügure 7.3b the velocities are given. From the ’¼ügure it is seen that the velocity in
the inlet channel decreases almost linearly. The opposite is seen for the outlet channel,
where a linear increase is seen. That the velocities change linearly can be explained by the
velocity in the wall. The velocity is not constant, but it only changes from its minimum
value at 1.76 cm/s to its maximum value at 1.79 cm/s. Compared to the value for the
channel velocities the velocity in the wall is thus almost constant.
That the two velocities for the channel are opposite is due to mass conservation. The
mass that is removed from the inlet channel must be added to the outlet channel. It is
assumed that the velocity in the z direction in the wall is zero, thereby the sum of the
two mass ’¼éow rate at the same z must be constant and equal to the inlet mass ’¼éow rate.
Because the density is constant, the sum of the velocity must also be constant, that this
is ful’¼ülled is also seen in ’¼ügure 7.3b.
The velocity in the wall is calculated from the pressure di’¼Ćerence (see equation (7.46)).
From ’¼ügure 7.3a, it is seen that the pressure di’¼Ćerence is largest at the two ends, the
velocity in the wall must thus be biggest at these ends, which is also seen in ’¼ügure 7.3b.
The wall velocity reaches a minimum in the middle of the channel, this is expected as
the pressure di’¼Ćerence is smallest in the middle.
The results are further investigated for the how high the number of grid points needs
to be, because a low number of grid points saves computational costs. The result will
also be compared with previous work (see section 7.3.2) and how the system reacted
to di’¼Ćerent volumetric ’¼éow rates into the system (section 7.3.3). At last, the result is
investigated for how sensitive the system is to parameter changes (section 7.3.4).
7.3.1
Number of Grid Points
The number of grid points in the channel, N, has a signi’¼ücant in’¼éuence on the number of
equations and variables to be solved for, but it also in’¼éuences how accurate the solution
of the model is. It is thus important to choose a value that gives an accurate result, but
still keeps the number of equations at a minimum. In ’¼ügure 7.4 the pressure and velocity
in the system are given for three di’¼Ćerent values of N.
44
==PAGE:55==
(a) The pressure in the channel
(b) The velocity in the channel and wall
Figure 7.4: The pressure and velocity in the channel for three di’¼Ćerent values of N: N = 5
(solid line), N = 25 (dashed line) and N = 100 (dotted line). Red indicates the inlet, blue the
outlet and black the wall. Gin = 8.56 ┬Ę 10ŌłÆ6 m3/s.
From the ’¼ügures it is not possible to see any di’¼Ćerence between velocity in the channel
for the di’¼Ćerent values of N, and only a small change is seen for the pressure in the
channel. However, a big di’¼Ćerence is seen for the gas velocity in the wall. It is di’¼ācult
to determine how many grid points are needed from the ’¼ügure, but it is seen that the
number of grid points has an in’¼éuence on the gas velocity in the wall. The di’¼Ćerence is
thus investigated by calculating the error between the result for N = 100 and N = X.
The error for the di’¼Ćerent pressures and velocities between the results are given in table
7.2.
Table 7.2: The error in percent between N = 100 and N = X for Gin = 8.56 ┬Ę 10ŌłÆ6 m3/s.

From the table it is seen that a higher value for N gives a smaller error, which is also
expected. From the table it is also seen that the biggest di’¼Ćerence is for the velocity in the
channel. The computational time for the simulation is not shown in the table, because
at this point the simulation only takes a few seconds for all values of N. However, as the
model becomes more complex the computational time will increase, and at that point
it is important to have a low number of grid points. It is thus decided to use N = 10,
which gives an error at 0.28 % for the velocity in the channel. A value of N = 5 also
gives an acceptable error, but N = 10 is chosen because it will give more smooth graphs.
However, it should be noted that N = 100 does not give the correct solution, but the
correct solution is reached when N goes to in’¼ünity, at the same time in the derivation of
the model equations some assumptions were introduced that will make the model itself
not accurate.
7.3.2
Comparison with Previous Work
The results that are discussed above are compared with previous simulation results. The
result will be compared with the results from the articles given in table 5.1.
45
==PAGE:56==
For the articles given in table 5.1 it is only Depcik and Assanis [38] that illustrated
the result for the pressure and velocities. The result from Depcik and Assanis [38] is
showed in ’¼ügure 7.5. For the result is assumed that there is no soot in the inlet and the
change in area is thus zero.
(a) Pressure in the channel and wall.
(b) Velocity in the channel and wall.
Figure 7.5: Results from Depcik and Assanis simulation of the ’¼éow[38].
If the ’¼ügure is compared with ’¼ügure 7.3 it is seen that both the inlet and outlet pressure
and velocity look qualitative the same. The velocity in the wall, however, does not. They
both have a parabolic velocity, but the di’¼Ćerence in the velocity at the two ends is much
higher in Depcik and AssanisŌĆÖ result [38] compared to the results for the model presented
above. The qualitative di’¼Ćerence is di’¼ācult to explain because of the missing information
of value for the permeability and density. There is also some missing information of the
exact model equations.
7.3.3
Responds to Di’¼Ćerent Volumetric Flow Rate
The system is further investigated for how it responds to di’¼Ćerent volumetric ’¼éow rates
into the system. The velocities and pressures for the original mass ’¼éow rate (8.56 ┬Ę 10ŌłÆ6
m3/s) and ┬▒10 % of the original value are shown in ’¼ügure 7.6.
46
==PAGE:57==
(a) Pressure in the channel.
(b) Velocities in the channel and wall.
Figure 7.6: The pressure and velocity for three di’¼Ćerent volumetric ’¼éow rate into the system:
8.56 ┬Ę 10ŌłÆ6 m3/s, the original value (dashed line), 10 % decrease of the original value (dotted
line), 10 % increase of the original value (solid line). Red indicates the inlet parameter, blue
the outlet and black the wall.
From the ’¼ügure it is seen that there is a linear correlation between the pressure and
the volumetric ’¼éow rate. The linear correlation is also seen for the velocities. That the
inlet velocity changes linearly with the volumetric ’¼éow rate into the system is expected,
because of the boundary conditions for uI at z = 0 (equation (7.19)). It is previously
discussed that the sum of the two channel mass ’¼éow rates is constant, therefore the
velocity at z = L must also change linearly with the mass ’¼éow rate into the system.
The velocity in the wall changes linearly with the mass ’¼éow rate, because the pressure
di’¼Ćerence changes linearly with the mass ’¼éow.
47
==PAGE:58==
7.3.4
Discussion of Parameter and Sensitivity Analyse
The input parameters for the equations can be divided in three parts: Filter dimensions
(L, d, tf), ’¼ülter parameters (K, F) and gas parameters (Žü, ┬Ą).
The ’¼ülter dimensions should be investigated if the system is optimized, but is considered
out of scope.
Filter Parameter
The ’¼ülter parameter depends on the ’¼ülter material. These parameters
are more di’¼ācult to measure. How the parameters in’¼éuence the result is thus investigated
by a sensitivity analysis. The system is solved with a ┬▒10 % change compared to the
original value given in table 7.1. In ’¼ügure 7.7 the result is given for a ┬▒10 % change in
permeability, K, and in ’¼ügure 7.8 the result is given for a ┬▒10 % in the friction factor,
F.
(a) Pressure in the channel
(b) Velocity in the channel and wall.
Figure 7.7: Pressures and velocities in the channel and wall for di’¼Ćerent permeability, K.
K = 1.17 ┬Ę 10ŌłÆ14 m2 (original value) dashed line, 10 % increase dotted line and 10 % decrease
solid line. Red indicates the inlet parameter, blue the outlet and black the wall. Gin = 8.56┬Ę10ŌłÆ6
m3/s.
(a) Pressure in the channel.
(b) Velocity in the channel and wall.
Figure 7.8: Pressures and velocities in the channel and wall for di’¼Ćerent friction factor, F.
F = 28.454 (original value) dashed line, 10 % increase dotted line and 10 % decrease solid line.
Red indicates the inlet parameter, blue the outlet and black the wall. Gin = 8.56 ┬Ę 10ŌłÆ6 m3/s.
The in’¼éuence on the pressure is given in ’¼ügure 7.7a and 7.8a. From ’¼ügure 7.7a, it is seen
that the pressure in the outlet channel is not in’¼éuenced by the changes in permeability
48
==PAGE:59==
where the pressure in the inlet increases linearly with the decrease in permeability. The
friction factor however in’¼éuences both the inlet and outlet pressure. From ’¼ügure 7.8a, it
is seen that the two pressures are in’¼éuenced the same way - an increase in the friction
factor gives a higher pressure in the channel.
From ’¼ügure 7.7b and 7.8b, it is seen that the velocities for the channel do not show
any reaction for the changes in the two parameters. The velocity in the wall however
changes, and thereby the velocities in the channel must be in’¼éuenced, but the in’¼éuence
cannot be seen graphically. The velocity in the wall increases at the two ends and de-
creases in the middle of the channel with an increase in the friction factor and by an
increase in the permeability.
Gas Parameter
The gas parameters are known parameters, which will depend on the
pressure and temperature. ┬Ą is only a function of the temperature and the change in
viscosity thus only needs to be included when the temperature is not constant. The
density however will change with the pressure (Žü = MP/(RT)). In ’¼ügure 7.3a, it is
seen that the pressure changes from 1.17 atm at the inlet to 1 atm at the outlet, this
gives a change at 17 %. The density thus changes with 17 % with a ’¼éow at 8.56 ┬Ę 10ŌłÆ6
m3/s. In ’¼ügure 7.9 the pressure and velocity in the channels are shown for a simulation
with constant density and where the density is given by the ideal gas law (the discretised
equations are given in appendix A.1 and in appendix B.2 the Matlab script for the density
as function of the pressure is given). When the density is a function of the pressure, the
velocity of the gas in the wall is not constant, the wall is therefore also discretized. How
the velocity and pressure changes with y is given in ’¼ügure 7.10. In ’¼ügure 7.9 and 7.10 the
system is solved with the parameters given in table 7.1 and with N = 10 and Nw = 15.
(a) The pressure in the channel.
(b) The velocity in the channel and wall.
Figure 7.9: The pressure and velocity in the channel. Dashed line is for constant density and
solid line is for density as function of the pressure. Red indicates the inlet parameter, blue the
outlet and black the wall. Gin = 8.56 ┬Ę 10ŌłÆ6 m3/s.
49
==PAGE:60==
(a) The pressure in the wall.
(b) The velocity in the wall.
Figure 7.10: The pressure and velocity in the wall at three di’¼Ćerent position in the channel.
The dashed line is for constant density and the solid line is for density as function of the pressure.
Red: z = 0.1L, blue: z = 0.5L, black z = L. Gin = 8.56 ┬Ę 10ŌłÆ6 m3/s
From the ’¼ügure 7.9, it is seen that the pressure and velocity in the outlet channel is
in’¼éuenced less than the pressure and velocity in the inlet channel. This is because the
constant density is closest to the real density for the outlet channel. A better ’¼üt for
the inlet channel can thus be obtained using another constant density, but this will also
in’¼éuence the outlet channel.
From ’¼ügure 7.10a it is seen how the pressure in the wall reacted to the assumption
of the constant density. From the ’¼ügure it is seen that the biggest change is the pressure
at y = 0, this change is due to the pressure in the inlet channel decreases when the dens-
ity is calculated by the ideal gas low. The pressure at y = tf is almost not in’¼éuenced,
because the pressure in the outlet channel is almost not in’¼éuenced.
The gas velocity in the wall is given in ’¼ügure 7.10b. From the ’¼ügure it is seen that
especially the velocity is in’¼éuenced by how the density is calculated. When the density is
assumed constant, the velocity will be constant at each grid point in the channel. How-
ever, if the density changes with the pressure, the velocity is not constant any more. The
velocity increases down the wall because the pressure decreases.
From the investigation of the assumption of the constant density, it can be concluded
that the density needs to be expressed by the ideal gas law because of the high pressure
di’¼Ćerences in the system.
50
==PAGE:61==
7.4
Sub-conclusion
For the above model, results and discussion of the results it is concluded that the model
reacted as expected. The pressure in the inlet channel is always higher than the pressure
in the outlet channel. Furthermore, both pressures decrease because of the friction down
the channel. For the velocities, the inlet velocity decreases down the channel and the
outlet velocity increases, this is also expected as the mass is removed from the inlet and
added to the outlet. The velocity in the wall is parabolic with a minimum in the middle
of the channel.
When the result was compared with previous work by Depcik and Assanis [38] there
was a good agreement between the results. The biggest di’¼Ćerence was for the velocity in
the wall. In Depcik and AssanisŌĆÖ work [38] the velocity also changed parabolic, but they
have a much bigger di’¼Ćerence between the two end velocities. This di’¼Ćerence can be due
to model parameters or model equations, it was not possible to determine the reason due
to lack of information.
It was further concluded that the density needs to be calculated by the ideal gas law
because of the large pressure di’¼Ćerence.
The next step will be step (2), where the ’¼éow model is solved with SCR kinetic. The
model is thus extended to also include the species balance.
51
==PAGE:62==
8
Flow Model with SCR Kinetic
The SCR kinetic model will be added to the ’¼éow model in this section. By doing so
the model can be used to predict the NOx conversion. The SCR kinetic model is added
to the system before the soot kinetic model, because the amount of soot deposited on
the wall changes with time. By adding the SCR kinetic model the system does thus not
change with time (the species balances are also assumed in state steady).
8.1
Model Derivation
The control volumes, which are used to derive the model are the same as for the ’¼éow
model (see ’¼ügure 7.1). Because the model is solved with the SCR kinetic the system will
have continuity, momentum and species balances. All three balances will be assumed in
state steady. The concentration of NOx is very low - typically less than 1000 ppmv; it
will thus be assumed that the molar mass of the gas is constant. The continuity and
momentum balance are thus the same as given in section 7.1.
8.1.1
Species Balances
Species balances will be set up for the relevant species in the SCR chemistry, i.e. NO, NO2,
N2O, NH3 and the coverage of NH3 on the catalyst surface. The oxygen concentration
is assumed constant, even though oxygen is consumed by the SCR reaction, because the
concentration is very high compared to the concentration of NOx. The balance for oxygen
will thus not be set up. The balance for all the species only varies by the reaction term.
It is thus possible to set up a general balance for all species.
Inlet Channel
For the species balance in the inlet channel there is an input at z = z,
an output at z = z + Ōłåz and some mass is transferred through the wall. The input and
output will be due to the concentration gradient and the convection. The species balance
for j in the inlet channel is given by:

8.2.1
Result and Discussion for Simple Reaction Rate
The system of equations is solved with the variables given in table 7.1, the rate constants
given in table 8.1 and with N = 10 and Nw = 15. The inlet concentrations are: 550
ppm NH3 and 500 ppm NO. With these variables the concentration of NO in the wall
and the outlet channel are given as in ’¼ügure 8.1. The concentration for the inlet channel
is not shown, because the concentration in the inlet channel is constant since the mass
transfer coe’¼ācient is zero, and equal to the concentration in the inlet. The pressures and
velocities are not shown either, because they are given in ’¼ügure 7.9 for the case where
the density is given by the ideal gas law.

The changes in concentra-
tion is calculated as the di’¼Ćerent in the concentration at y = 0 and at y = tf. It is seen
that the conversion of NO is greatest in the middle of the channel and lower at the two

greatest in the ends. The same pro’¼üle is seen for NH3. From the ’¼ügure it is also seen
that the amount of mol NO reacted is equal to the amount of mol NH3 reacted. This is
also expected as one mol NO reacted with one mol NH3 (see reaction R22). The total
conversion of NO is 67.2 %.
In ’¼ügure 8.1c and 8.1d the concentration of NO and NH3 in the outlet channel is shown.
From the ’¼ügure it is seen that the concentration decreases down the channel up to a
little above the middle where the concentration starts to increase. The decreases in the
concentration is due to the concentration at y = tf is smaller than the concentration in
the channel. The opposite is the case when the concentration increases.
Number of Grid Points
The number of grid points in the channel is not investigated
at this point. A high number of grid points is important when the gradient is large. The
gradient for the species in the inlet channel is zero. And the gradient for the concentration
in the outlet channel is small compare with the gradient for the velocity. The number of
grid points in the y direction is thus the only one that is investigated.

Initial the equations are solved where only standard SCR, absorption and desorption of

are ful’¼ülled.

From ’¼ügure 8.6a it is seen that the concentration in the inlet channel decreases down the
channel when mass transfer due to concentration gradient is included. This is expected
because the concentration in the wall is smaller than the concentration in the channel.
Thus, the mass transfer due to concentration gradient increases the amount of j trans-
ferred to the wall.
The concentration in the outlet channel is seen in ’¼ügure 8.6b. In the ’¼ügure it is seen
that the concentration decreases down the channel. This is due to the concentration in
the wall at all y decrease down the channel. The concentration in the wall at y = tf will
thus always be lower than or equal to the concentration in the outlet channel. When
the mass transfer due to concentration gradient was neglected, an increase in the con-
centration in the outlet channel was seen in the end. This increase was due to the fact
that the concentration in the wall was higher than the concentration in the outlet channel.

seen when di’¼Ćusion is included.

69
==PAGE:80==
8.4
Sub-conclusion
In the above sections a ’¼éow model with SCR kinetic is derived and investigated by dif-
ferent operation conditions. The conditions can be divided in three overall cases: 1)
the NOx was only consumed by standard SCR and with di’¼Ćusion neglected, 2) all SCR
reactions were included and di’¼Ćusion was neglected and 3) with all reactions and with
di’¼Ćusion.
From the results where the NOx was only consumed by standard SCR it was seen that
the amount of consumed NO was equal to the amount of NH3 consumed. This is because
the stoichiometric between NO and NH3 is 1:1 in the standard SCR reaction.
For the simulation results for the second case, where all reactions were included, an
increase in conversion was seen compared to the simulation results where only standard
SCR reaction was considered. The increase in conversion is because of the fast SCR
reaction.
When di’¼Ćusion was included (case 3) the conversion increased even more. Furthermore,
when di’¼Ćusion is included the concentration in the inlet channel decreases down the
channel, where when di’¼Ćusion was neglected, the concentration in the inlet channel was
constant. Overall, di’¼Ćusion in’¼éuences the results signi’¼ücantly and should thus be in-
cluded in the model.
For all cases, an increase in conversion was seen when the temperature was increased. The
increase in conversion is because of the increase in reaction rate. Furthermore, when all
reactions are included an increase in conversion is seen when the NO/NO2 ratio increases
from 10 to 1.
70
==PAGE:81==
9
Flow Model with SCR and Soot Kinetic
In this section, the soot kinetic is added. The model thus goes from being a steady state
model to a transient model because the soot thickness changes with time. Furthermore,
with the addition of the soot layer to the model the system has a domain more. The
system can thus be divided in four parts: The inlet channel, the soot layer, the wall and
the outlet channel.
The model equations that are derived in section 7.1 and 8.1 deviate a bit when the
soot is added to the system. How each of the equations change is given in section 9.1.
9.1
Model Derivation
As mentioned above by accounting for the soot, the system will have an extra domain.
The system and the control volumes for each domain is given in ’¼ügure 9.1.
(a) The whole system, where the grey part is the wall and the green
parts are the soot layer.
(b) A zoom in on the soot layer.
(c) A zoom in on the wall.
Figure 9.1: The system with the control volumes and some of boundary conditions. The blue
parts are the system and boundary conditions, and the black parts are the coordinate system. P
is the pressure, u the velocity, d the diameter and Gin the volumetric ’¼éow rate into the system.
From the ’¼ügure, it is seen that the inlet channel now has a ’¼éow to the soot layer, instead
of a ’¼éow to the wall.
Furthermore, it is seen that the diameter of the inlet channel
decreases when soot is deposited. This change in diameter is neglected in the model.
The outlet channel still has a ’¼éow from the wall to the channel. Furthermore, it is seen
that the wall now has a ’¼éow from the soot layer to the wall, instead of from the inlet
channel. How this a’¼Ćected the model equations are given in the below sections.
9.1.1
Continuity Balance
Inlet Channel
For the continuity balance for the inlet channel, the changes in mass
’¼éow due to the deposited soot will be neglected. Furthermore, it is seen in ’¼ügure 9.1a
71
==PAGE:82==
that the channel diameter changes when soot is deposited on the wall. This change in
thickness will be neglected. From the literature study it was also found that Depcik and
Assanis concluded that if the model is used for normal operating conditions, the change
in area can be neglected (see section 5.3) [38]. Thus, the continuity balance for the inlet
channel does not deviate much from equation 7.5. The di’¼Ćerence between the equations
are that the ’¼éow goes to the soot layer instead of the wall. The continuity balance will
thus now be given by:
0 = ŌłÆd

The boundary conditions for this equation are still given by equation (7.19) and (7.20).
Outlet Channel
The balance for the outlet channel does not deviate from equation
(7.5). The boundary condition does not changes either; the boundary condition is thus
given by equation (7.21).
Wall
The soot that is deposited will at ’¼ürst be loaded in the wall. The porosity for the
wall thus decreases. Furthermore, the combustion of the soot will occur in the wall and
in the soot layer. However, in the model it will be assumed that the soot is only loaded
outside of the wall. The change in porosity and the combustion of soot in the wall will
thus be neglected.
With thus assumption the continuity balance for the wall does not change. The con-
tinuity equation is thus given by equation (7.12). However, at this point it is possible to

9.1.4

The soot layer will further have one more balance, a mass balance for the layer. The
mass balance for the soot layer is used to calculate how much soot is deposited on the
’¼ülter.

amount of soot that is removed by combustion. The mass balance does thus become:

9.1.5
Reaction Rate for the Soot Kinetic
The reaction rate for combustion of soot depends on whether the reaction is catalytic or
non-catalytic. The soot that is in contact with the wall will be oxidized catalytically, and
the soot that is not will be oxidized non-catalytic. To be able to describe the combustion
of soot, two soot kinetic models are thus needed. When each of the soot models are
used is distinguished by the so called two layer model [25] (the model is also presented
in section 2.1). The model is illustrated in ’¼ügure 9.3.
75
==PAGE:86==
Figure 9.3: Schematic illustration of the two-layer model [25].
The soot in layer 1 is a’¼Ćected by the catalyst, while the soot in layer 2 is not. In layer
1 both catalytic and non-catalytic combustion of soot takes place. The thickness of each
of the layers depends on the thickness of the soot layer. The thicknesses are de’¼üned as:
ts,1 =
’Ż▒
’Ż▓
’Ż│
ts
,
ts Ōēżts,sat
ts,sat

(9.22)

’Ż▒

0

ts Ōēżts,sat
ts ŌłÆts,sat

ts > ts,sat
(9.23)
Where ts,sat is the thickness of the maximum amount of soot that can be in layer 1. ts,sat
is a ’¼ütting parameter, that will depend on the surface of the wall. In the model, it is
assumed that all the soot in layer 1 is in contact with the catalyst.
Catalytic Combustion
The reaction for the catalytic combustion is given by [48]:
rsoot,cat = kcat(1 ŌłÆX)2/3
(9.24)
Where X is the conversion of soot. The reaction rate thus depends on the conversion
of the soot. The reaction rate depends on the conversion because it is a gas-solid-solid
reaction. The reaction occurs at the interface between the soot and the catalyst, and as
the conversion of soot increases the surface area increases. It is not possible to keep track
of the conversion, because ŌĆÖnewŌĆÖ soot is loaded on the wall. Therefore, in the calculations
the conversion for the soot will be set to zero. The kinetic parameters for the reaction
are given in table 9.1.
Table 9.1: Reaction rate and kinetic parameters for the catalyzed soot combustion. R is the

contact with a concentration of oxygen at 10.2 vol% in N2 [48].

surface, and the total catalyst area will not be covered by soot. At this point, the interface
will however be calculated by equation (9.25).

9.2
Solution with Fixed Soot Layer Thickness
It is decided to initially solve the equation with a ’¼üxed soot layer thickness (ts), by doing
so all the equations are in steady state. The discretization of the steady state equations
for the soot layer are given in appendix A.4, and in appendix B.5 the Matlab scripts are
given. The discretization of inlet channel, outlet channel and wall is not shown, because
of the small changes from the previous equations.
9.2.1

and 9.2. This is done because it is thus possible to check the mass balance. In this case,
the amount of generated NO is equal to the amount of consumed NO2. In ’¼ügure 9.4
the concentration in the inlet channel, outlet channel and in the wall and soot layer is
given. Furthermore, the amount of generated/consumed NO/NO2 is also shown. The
results given in ’¼ügure 9.4 is solved with the parameters given in table 7.1, 9.3 and the
di’¼Ćusion coe’¼ācients given in table 8.2. The number of grid points in the four domains
are: N = 10, Nw = 20 and Ns = 15.

As for the temperature, an increase in NO2 concentration gives an increase in the amount
