==PAGE:95==
9.3
Transient Solution
In this section all the model equations will be solved. The system will thus be transient,
because the thickness of the soot layer changes with time. The system will thus have
ordinary di’¼Ćerential equations and a partial di’¼Ćerential equation. How the system is
solved is explained in the section below.
9.3.1
Solution Strategy
The ordinary di’¼Ćerential equations are as before solved by discretization the space dimen-
sion, thereby they become algebraic equations. The time dimension is not discretization,
and the partial di’¼Ćerential equation is thereby given by an ordinary di’¼Ćerential equation.
The algebraic equations are still solved with MatlabŌĆÖs built in function fsolve where
the di’¼Ćerential equation is solved with the built in function ode15s. The set up of the
equations are given in ’¼ügure 9.8.
Figure 9.8: A breakdown of the functions in Matlab.
In the ’¼ügure it is seen that the main script (Flow_SCR_soot_model.m) gives the ini-
tial value for the thickness of the soot layer.
The main function also calls the func-
tion Flow_SCR_soot_model_fun.m which calculated the initial concentrations, pres-
sures and velocities with use of the built in function fsolve.
Afterwards the function
thickness.m is called, which calculates the thickness of the soot layer with use of the
built in function ode15s. For each time step the concentrations, pressures and velocities
needs to be calculated. The function thickness.m does thus also need to call the function
Flow_SCR_soot_model_fun.m.
85
==PAGE:96==
Calculation of the Soot Layer Thickness
The thickness of the soot layer is as men-
tioned calculated from the mass balance of the ’¼ülter. The mass balance for the ’¼ülter is
a di’¼Ćerential equation with respect to time and is thus therefore solved with the ode15s
solver. The thickness of the soot layer is not constant down the channel, and the thick-
ness is thus calculated for each grid point in the channel. There will thus be a total of N
di’¼Ćerential equations. The di’¼Ćerential equation can be written as:

9.3.2
Results and Discussion
The change in thickness over time is, as for the other results, investigated for both di’¼Ćerent
temperatures and NO/NO2 ratio. In all cases the initial thickness is 75 ┬Ąm. For all cases
the thickness does not reach a steady state. How the thickness changes with time is given
in appendix C. The change in thickness for all the cases, was very small, and thereby is
the change in generated CO, pressure drop and NOx conversion also very small; these
results are also given in appendix C.
In’¼éuence on Reaction Temperature
The thickness of the soot layer after one hour for
a temperature at 500 K and 600 K and after 20 min for a temperature at 700 K are given
in ’¼ügure 9.9.
Figure 9.9: The thickness of the soot layer after one hour for a temperature at 500 K (blue
dashed line) and 600 K (red solid line) and after 20 min for a temperature at 700 K (black
dotted line). The green solid line is the initial soot layer thickness.
From the ’¼ügure it is seen that for a temperature at 700 K the thickness decreases at all z.
The thickness decreases fastest in the beginning of the channel. This was also expected
because it was found that the amount of combusted soot is largest in the beginning of
the channel (see ’¼ügure 9.6).
For a temperature at 500 K and 600 K it is seen that
the thickness decreases in the beginning of the channel and increases in the end of the
channel. From the ’¼ügure it is further seen that the thickness for the soot layer decreases
with increasing temperature because of the increase in the reaction rate.

In’¼éuence on NO2 Concentration

From the ’¼ügure it is again seen that the thickness is smallest in the beginning and that
the amount of soot combusted increases with increasing NO2 concentration. Furthermore
is it seen that the amount of soot combusted at the end of the channel is more of less the
same for all NO2 concentrations.
87
==PAGE:98==
9.4
Sub-conclusion
The model with both the SCR and soot kinetic is investigated for two overall cases, one
where the soot layer was constant and one where the soot layer changes with time.
The ’¼ürst case was used to investigate how the concentration and pressure is in’¼éuenced.
The system was solved with a constant soot layer thickness of 75 ┬Ąm. From the simula-
tion results it was found that when the soot was added to the system the pressure drop
increased, this is expected as the there is also resistance in the soot layer. Furthermore
it was found that the conversion of NOx decreases when the soot kinetic is part of the
model. This was also expected because the soot kinetic consumes some of the NO2, and
thereby decreases the rate of the fast SCR reaction. The simulation was performed for
di’¼Ćerent temperature and NO/NO2 ratio, and in all cases it was found that as the tem-
perature or/and the NO2 concentration is increased, the amount of combusted soot and
the NOx conversion increases. It was also found that the combustion of soot is fastest in
the beginning of the channel.
In the second case, where the soot layer thickness changes with time, the simulation
was performed with an initial thickness of 75 ┬Ąm and the simulation was run for one hour
and one case for only 20 min. In all cases the simulation did not reach a steady state.
From these simulations it was also seen that the combustion of soot was fastest in the
beginning of the channel, leading to a smaller thickness in the beginning of the channel.
88
==PAGE:99==
10
Discussion
Mathematical modelling is a useful tool, which is widely used to predict and understand
how a system reacts to di’¼Ćerent operating conditions. However, when a model is de-
rived some assumptions need to be taken. The model will thereby only give an indication
of how the system reacts to changes. Among the assumptions used in this model, is that
the combustion and loading of the soot is only on the wall and not in the wall. Another
assumption that was used is that the ’¼éow in the solid layer is only in the radial direction
and not in the axial direction. Both of these assumptions are used because they simplify
the equations and the implements of the equations in Matlab.
In reality the soot is also loaded in the wall, because of the porous medium and thereby
the combustion of the soot will also occur in the wall. However, it is only a small amount
of soot that can be loaded in the ’¼ülter because of the small volume. It is thus believed
that the assumption is valid. Furthermore, the soot combustion kinetic have two ’¼ütting
parameters, the height of the soot that is a’¼Ćected by the catalyst and the maximal surface
area between the catalyst and the soot. These two parameter can be used to ’¼üt experi-
mental data to the amount of combusted soot, and thereby overcome the error from the
fact that the combustion also occurs in the wall.
The second assumption that is highlighted is more di’¼ācult to validate or dismiss. From
the simulation it was found that the velocity was highest at the two ends and smallest
in the middle. However, the di’¼Ćerence was very small, and it is thus believed that it
is a good assumption to neglect the ’¼éow in the axial direction. For the species balance
it is another case. The concentration of the species is highest in the beginning of the
channel, and decreases down the channel. There will therefore be di’¼Ćusion both in the
radial direction and the axial direction. It is di’¼ācult to implement the di’¼Ćusion in the
axial direction because of two reasons. When the soot layer thickness changes with time,
the thickness will not be constant down the channel, the node points down the soot layer
will thus not be located at the same axial position. Furthermore, if di’¼Ćusion in the axial
direction is included, the species balance will be much more complicated, and it will be
even more di’¼ācult for the solver to ’¼ünd a solution. Whether the changes in the axial
direction are needed to have a model that can predict how the system reacted must be
investigated by comparing the simulation results with experimental data.
At this point the model is not validated with experimental data. Comparison with ex-
perimental data is the only way it is possible to see whether the model can be used to
predict how the system reacts. Furthermore, experimental data should also be used to ’¼üt
the model parameters and thereby get the best ’¼üt between the model and experimental
data. Even though the model is not validated with experimental data, the model seems
like it reacts as it should. As the temperature and the NO2 concentration increase both
the amount of soot combusted and the NOx concentration increase. Furthermore, the
NOx conversion decreases as soot is loaded on the wall. These observations are also seen
by experimental data. However, whether the exact value is reasonable a more detailed
comparison is needed.
The model that is derived and solved is an isothermal model. The model can thus only be
validated with experiment where the temperature is kept constant. The model can thus
not be used in real cases, because there the temperature will not be constant. However,
the model can be expanded to also include the change in temperature. However, it is
89
==PAGE:100==
suggested that the model is at ’¼ürst validated with isothermal experimental data. Because
if the model does not ’¼üt with the data for the isothermal experiment, the model will not
’¼üt data from non-isothermal data either.
As mentioned the model is at this point only an isothermal model, and can thereby
only be used to predict the response to isothermal data. Even though the model is iso-
thermal the model can still be very useful, if it ’¼üts experimental data. At this point the
model can be used to predict the pressure drop and NOx conversion, which are two para-
meters that are important for the DPF combined with the SCR catalyst. Furthermore,
the model can also be used to predict the optimal NO2 concentration at di’¼Ćerent tem-
peratures and NOx concentration. At this point the system is solved with an NO/NO2
ratio at 10, 4 and 1 with a total NOx concentration at 550 ppm. From these simulation
results it was found that a ratio of 1 for all cases gave the highest conversion. However,
the model will have an optimal ratio, because of the slow SCR reaction that consume NO2.
The concentration of NH3 is also an parameter that can be optimised. However, the
concentration of NH3 is more complex and therefore all the simulations are made for a
NH3 concentration at 550 ppm. The NH3 concentration is more complex because a high
concentration of NH3 gives a high NOx conversion, but at the same time the concentra-
tion of NH3 in the outlet of the ’¼ülter increases. The concentration of NH3 in the outlet
must be kept low, to keep the consumption of NH3 to a minimum, but also because
NH3 is reduced to N2 in the ammonia slip catalyst, which is placed after the ’¼ülter. The
model can thereby not be used to ’¼ünd the optimal NH3 concentration, but the model can
be used to ’¼ünd the needed NH3 concentration to ful’¼üll the NOx emission regulation for
di’¼Ćerent NOx concentration and temperatures.
90
==PAGE:101==
11
Conclusion
This thesis has worked with modelling of a diesel particle ’¼ülter (DPF) which is used to
remove soot from exhaust gas. The ’¼ülter is coated with a catalyst, which reduces the
NOx concentration by the so called selective catalyst reduction (SCR). The ’¼ülter which
is modelled is thus a combined DPF-SCR ’¼ülter. The model that is derived and solved
in this thesis is an isothermal model. The solution to the model equations are found by
dividing the model in smaller sub-models. The sub-models are: 1) a ’¼éow model which
calculated the velocity and pressure, 2) a ’¼éow model with SCR kinetic and 3) a ’¼éow
model with SCR and soot kinetic.
From the ’¼ürst model (the ’¼éow model) it was found that the pressure decreases down
the channel for both the inlet and outlet channel. Furthermore, it was found that the
pressure in the inlet channel is higher than the outlet channel due to the resistance in
the wall. For the velocity it was found that the velocity decreases away from the inlet
and outlet of the channel. For the velocity of the gas in the wall it was found that the
velocity was highest in the two ends and lowest in the middle.
In the second model (the ’¼éow model with SCR kinetic) the species balance was included
to the model equations. The species balance was solved where di’¼Ćusion was neglected
and where the di’¼Ćusion was included. From the simulation results it was found that
di’¼Ćusion has a great in’¼éuence on the concentration pro’¼üle. If di’¼Ćusion is neglected, the
concentration in the inlet channel is constant, where when di’¼Ćusion is included the con-
centration decreases down the channel. Without di’¼Ćusion the NOx conversion at each
point in the channel was almost constant, but highest in the middle because the resident
time was highest.
When di’¼Ćusion was included the concentration was highest in the
beginning of the channel, and thereby the reaction rate was also higher in the beginning
of the channel. When di’¼Ćusion was included an increase in the NOx conversion was seen
compared to the results without di’¼Ćusion.
In the third and last model the soot kinetic was added. The model can thus be used
to predict the NOx conversion and the amount of soot combusted, and thereby the thick-
ness of the soot layer.
From the simulation results it was found that the conversion
of NOx decreases when soot was added. The decrease in conversion is because NO2 is
consumed in the soot kinetic, leading to a decrease in the fast SCR reaction. From the
results it was also found that the combustion of soot was highest in the beginning of
the channel, and thereby the soot layer thickness will be lowest in the beginning of the
channel.

Wall
When the density was constant the velocity in the wall was also constant due to
equation (7.11), but if the density is calculated as function of the pressure the velocity
will not be constant any more. In this case it is necessary to discretized the wall both in
the y and z direction. In the following equations the grid point in the z direction is not
speci’¼üed, but they are all for i = i.

C
Simulation Results
C.1
Flow model with SCR and Soot kinetic
In ’¼ügure C.1 the thickness of the soot layer at three di’¼Ćerent position in the channel is
shown as function of time. From the ’¼ügure it is seen that none of the simulation results
reached a steady state. The NOx conversion, CO concentration in the outlet and the
pressure drop do thus either not reach a steady state as seen in ’¼ügure C.2, C.3 and C.4.
(a) T = 700 K, NO/NO2 = 10.
(b) T = 600 K, NO/NO2 = 10.
(c) T = 500 K, NO/NO2 = 10.
(d) T = 600 K, NO/NO2 = 4.
(e) T = 600 K, NO/NO2 = 1.
Figure C.1: The soot layer thickness as function of time. Black solid line is for z = L, blue
dashed line is for z = 0.5L and red dotted line is for z = 0.1L.
Gin = 8.56 ┬Ę 10ŌłÆ6 m3/s,
YceNH3 = 550 ppm and YNOx = 550 ppm.
How the NOx conversion changes with time is given in ’¼ügure C.2. From the ’¼ügure it is
seen that the NOx conversion increases in all cases except for the temperature at 500 K.
From the ’¼ügure it is also seen that the change in NOx conversion is very small, which is
because of the small change in thickness.
